<NYT_BYLINE >
<h6 class="byline">By 
<span itemprop="author creator" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person" itemid="http://topics.nytimes.com/top/reference/timestopics/people/f/martin_fackler/index.html">
<a href="http://topics.nytimes.com/top/reference/timestopics/people/f/martin_fackler/index.html" rel="author" title="More Articles by MARTIN FACKLER"><span itemprop="name">MARTIN FACKLER</span></a></span></h6>
</NYT_BYLINE>
<h6 class="dateline">Published: March 11, 2011    </h6>
<div class="shareTools shareToolsThemeClassic articleShareToolsTop"
	data-shares="facebook,twitter,google,save,email,showall|Share,print,singlepage,reprints,ad"

	data-url="http://www.nytimes.com/2011/03/12/world/asia/12japan.html"

<meta name="emailThisHash" content="tXW7rwmGxUWNrEnGNsAoug">
<div class="articleBody">
<span itemprop="copyrightHolder provider sourceOrganization" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" itemid="http://www.nytimes.com">
<meta itemprop="name" content="The New York Times Company"/>
<meta itemprop="url" content="http://www.nytco.com/"/>
<meta itemprop="tickerSymbol" content="NYSE NYT"/>
</span>
<meta itemprop="copyrightYear" content="2011"/>







<NYT_TEXT >

<NYT_CORRECTION_TOP>
</NYT_CORRECTION_TOP>
    <p itemprop="articleBody">
TOKYO — Rescuers struggled to reach survivors on Saturday morning as <a href="http://topics.nytimes.com/top/news/international/countriesandterritories/japan/index.html?inline=nyt-geo" title="More news and information about Japan." class="meta-loc">Japan</a> reeled after an earthquake and a tsunami struck in deadly tandem. The 8.9-magnitude earthquake set off a devastating tsunami that sent walls of water washing over coastal cities in the north. Concerns mounted over possible radiation leaks from two nuclear plants near the earthquake zone.        </p> 
</div>

<div class="columnGroup doubleRule"><div class="story">
<h5><a href="http://submit.nytimes.com/tsunami-earthquake"><img src="http://graphics8.nytimes.com/images/multimedia/icons/photo_icon.gif" alt="Photographs" width="12" height="10"> Are You at the Scene? Send In Your Photos</a></h5>

<div class="inlineImage module">
<div class="image">
<div class="icon enlargeThis"><a href="javascript:pop_me_up2('http://www.nytimes.com/imagepages/2011/03/12/jp-japan-1.html','jp_japan_1_html','width=720,height=586,scrollbars=yes,toolbars=no,resizable=yes')">Enlarge This Image</a></div>
<a href="javascript:pop_me_up2('http://www.nytimes.com/imagepages/2011/03/12/jp-japan-1.html','jp_japan_1_html','width=720,height=586,scrollbars=yes,toolbars=no,resizable=yes')">
<span itemprop="associatedMedia" itemscope itemid="http://graphics8.nytimes.com/images/2011/03/12/world/jp-japan-1/jp-japan-1-articleInline.jpg" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject">
<img itemprop="url" src="http://graphics8.nytimes.com/images/2011/03/12/world/jp-japan-1/jp-japan-1-articleInline.jpg" width="190" height="133" alt="">
<meta itemprop="identifier" content="http://graphics8.nytimes.com/images/2011/03/12/world/jp-japan-1/jp-japan-1-articleInline.jpg" />
<meta itemprop="height" content="190" />
<meta itemprop="width" content="133" />
<meta itemprop="copyrightHolder" content="Kyodo News, via Reuters" />
</span>
</a>
</div>
<h6 class="credit">Kyodo News, via Reuters</h6>
<p class="caption">Evacuees gathered on Friday in Shinjuku Central Park in Tokyo, hit hard even though it was far from the epicenter.                            <a href="/slideshow/2011/03/12/world/asia/20110312_JAPAN.html" onClick="javascript:s_code_linktrack('Article-MorePhotos');">More Photos &#x00bb;</a>
</p>
</div>
  
</div>
<div id="readerscomment" class="inlineLeft"></div>
<div class="articleBody">
 <p itemprop="articleBody">
The death toll from the tsunami and earthquake, the strongest ever recorded in Japan, was in the hundreds, but Japanese news media quoted government officials as saying that it would almost certainly rise to more than 1,000. About 200 to 300 bodies were found along the waterline in Sendai, a port city in northeastern Japan and the closest major city to the epicenter.        </p><p itemprop="articleBody">
Thousands of homes were destroyed, many roads were impassable, trains and buses were not running, and power and cellphones remained down. On Saturday morning, the JR rail company said that there were three trains missing in parts of two northern prefectures.        </p><p itemprop="articleBody">
While the loss of life and property may yet be considerable, many lives were certainly saved by Japan’s extensive disaster preparedness and strict construction codes. Japan’s economy was spared a more devastating blow because the earthquake hit far from its industrial heartland.        </p><p itemprop="articleBody">
Japanese officials on Saturday issued broad evacuation orders for people living in the vicinity of two separate nuclear power plants that had experienced breakdowns in their cooling systems as a result of the earthquake, and they warned that small amounts of radiation could leak from both plants.        </p><p itemprop="articleBody">
On Friday, at 2:46 p.m. Tokyo time, the quake struck. First came the roar and rumble of the temblor, shaking skyscrapers, toppling furniture and buckling highways. Then waves as high as 30 feet rushed onto shore, whisking away cars and carrying blazing buildings toward factories, fields and highways.        </p><p itemprop="articleBody">
By Saturday morning, Japan was filled with scenes of desperation, as stranded survivors called for help and rescuers searched for people buried in the rubble. Kazushige Itabashi, an official in Natori City, one of the areas hit hardest by the tsunami, said several districts in an area near Sendai’s airport were annihilated.        </p><p itemprop="articleBody">
Rescuers found 870 people in one elementary school on Saturday morning and were trying to reach 1,200 people in the junior high school, closer to the water. There was no electricity and no water for people in shelters. According to a newspaper, the Mainichi Shimbun, about 600 people were on the roof of a public grade school, in Sendai City. By Saturday morning, Japan’s Self-Defense Forces and firefighters had evacuated about 150 of them.        </p><p itemprop="articleBody">
On the rooftop of Chuo Hospital in the city of Iwanuma, doctors and nurses were waving white flags and pink umbrellas, according to TV Asahi. On the floor of the roof, they wrote “Help” in English, and “Food” in Japanese. The reporter, observing the scene from a helicopter, said, “If anyone in the City Hall office is watching, please help them.”        </p><p itemprop="articleBody">
The station also showed scenes of people stranded on a bridge, cut off by water on both sides near the mouth of the Abukuma River in Miyagi Prefecture.        </p><p itemprop="articleBody">
People were frantically searching for their relatives. Fumiaki Yamato, 70, was in his second home in a mountain village outside of Sendai when the earthquake struck. He spoke from his car as he was driving toward Sendai trying to find the rest of his family. While it usually takes about an hour to drive to the city, parts of the road were impassable. “I’m getting worried,” he said as he pulled over to take a reporter’s call. “I don’t know how many hours it’s going to take.”        </p><p itemprop="articleBody">
Japanese, accustomed to frequent earthquakes, were stunned by this one’s magnitude and the more than 100 aftershocks, many equivalent to major quakes.        </p><p itemprop="articleBody">
“I never experienced such a strong earthquake in my life,” said Toshiaki Takahashi, 49, an official at Sendai City Hall. “I thought it would stop, but it just kept shaking and shaking, and getting stronger.”        </p><p itemprop="articleBody">
Train service was shut down across central and northern Japan, including Tokyo, and air travel was severely disrupted.        </p>
         <noscript>
             <img src="http://meter-svc.nytimes.com/meter.gif"/>
         </noscript>

            title="Next Page"
            href="/2011/03/12/world/asia/12japan.html?pagewanted=2">Next Page &#x00bb;</a></div><NYT_AUTHOR_ID>	<div class="authorIdentification">

        <script type="text/template" id="playlist-item-tmpl">
        
        
	
		
		{% for video in videos %}
	        {% set visible_state = 'hide' %}
	        {% set img_src_attr = 'src' %}
	        {% if sponsorPlaylist %}
			{% set video_url = video.seo_url|playlistOverride(video.playlist.id) %}
			{% else %}
			{% set video_url = video.seo_url|playlistOverride(playlist.id) %}
			{% endif %}
	  
  
	

	{% if video.playlist.type|getPlaylistType() === "show" %}
		{% set inChannel = false %}
		{% set inShow = true %}
	{% else %}
		{% if video.playlist.type|getPlaylistType() === "channel" %}
			{% set inChannel = true %}
			{% set inShow = false %}
		{% else %}
			{% set inChannel = false %}
			{% set inShow = false %}
		{% endif %}
	{% endif %}

	{% set itemPropURL = '' %}
	{% set itemPropContentURL = '' %}
	{% set itemPropName = '' %}
	{% set itemPropListElement = '' %}
	{% set itemScope = '' %}
	{% if pageType !== "watch" %}
		{% set itemPropURL = 'itemprop=url' %}
		{% set itemPropContentURL = 'itemprop=contentUrl' %}
		{% set itemPropName = 'itemprop=name' %}
		{% set itemPropListElement = 'itemprop=associatedMedia' %}
		{% set itemScope = 'itemscope itemtype=http://schema.org/ImageObject' %}
	{% endif %}

	<li data-id="{{ video.id }}"
		class="thumb-item {{ visible_state }} {% if !hideShowKicker %}show-enabled{% endif %} {{video.assetMeta.name}} {% if inShow %}channel-name-{{ video.playlist.parent_channel.headline | slugify | default('default') }} channel-id-{{ video.playlist.parent_channel.id }} {% else %} channel-name-default {% endif %} channel-themed" {{ itemPropListElement }} {{ itemScope }} >

		{% if !hideShowKicker %}
		<span class="show-logo">
			{% if inShow %}<a class="show-name-link" href="{{ video.playlist.publish_url|getPlaylistLabel() }}">{{ video.playlist.headline }}</a>{% else %}<span class="show-name-link">&nbsp;</span>{% endif %}
		</span>
		{% endif %}
		<a data-collection="{{playlist.headline}}" class="thumb-holder" href="{{ video_url }}" {{ itemPropURL }}>
			<span class="thumb-wrapper">
				<span class="thumb-border"></span>
				<span class="thumb-crop">
					<img class="thumb-img" alt="{{ video.headline }}" title="{{ video.headline }}" {{ img_src_attr }}="{{ 'images'|getDomain }}{{ video.images | getThumbImage(moduleType, pageType, default_images) }}" {{ itemPropContentURL }} />
				</span>
			</span>
			<div class="play-holder">
				<p class="button-play"><i class="times-sprite icon {{video.assetMeta.name}}"></i></p>
			</div>
		</a>
		{# To do using video metadata add link to the channel/section #}
		{% if !hideChannelKicker %}
		<h5 class="thumb-section-name">
			{% if inShow %}
				<a class="section-name-link" href="{{ video.playlist.parent_channel.publish_url|getPlaylistLabel() }}">{{ video.playlist.parent_channel.headline }}</a>
			{% else %}
				<a class="section-name-link" href="{{ video.playlist.publish_url|getPlaylistLabel() }}">{{ video.playlist.headline }}</a>
			{% endif %}

			{% if (video.duration > 0) %}
				<span class="duration">{{ video.duration | formatDuration }}</span>
			{% endif %}
		</h5>
		{% endif %}
		<a data-collection="{{playlist.headline}}" class="thumb-headline-holder" href="{{ video_url }}">
			<h3 class="thumb-headline" {{ itemPropName }}>{{ video.headline }}</h3>
		</a>
	</li>
	

	
		
			{% endfor %}
		
	


        
        </script>

            <script type="text/javascript">
                window.BackboneLocal = window.Backbone;
                var NYTD = NYTD || {};
                NYTD.env = "production";
                function getAppSettings(){
    var playlistType = "show";
    var playlistHeadline = "T On Set";
    var playlistDisplayName = "T Magazine";
    var channelName = (playlistType === 'show' && playlistHeadline !== "") ? playlistDisplayName : playlistHeadline;

    var AppSettings = {
        domain: {
            base: "http://www.nytimes.com",
            js: "http://js.nyt.com/video/4.7.17-638_1447954390_2015-11-19T17:33:10Z/static-timesvideo",
            jsSecure: "https://sv.nyt.com/video/4.7.17-638_1447954390_2015-11-19T17:33:10Z/static-timesvideo",
            jsLegacy: "http://js.nyt.com",
            css: "http://css.nyt.com/video/4.7.17-638_1447954390_2015-11-19T17:33:10Z/static-timesvideo",
            cssLegacy: "http://css.nyt.com",

<meta name="summary" content="<p><a href=http://topics.nytimes.com/topics/reference/timestopics/people/k/david_kocieniewski/index.html>David Kocieniewski</a>, a business reporter for The New York Times, devoted a year to digging out and exposing the obscure provisions that businesses and the wealthiest Americans exploit to drive their tax bills down to rock bottom.</p>
<p>In a series called &#8220;But Nobody Pays That,&#8221; Mr. Kocieniewski showed how federal tax law takes with one hand but gives &#8212; generously &#8212; with the other.</p>
<p>At 35 percent, the corporate tax rate is nominally among the world&#8217;s highest. Yet because of &#8220;a bounty of subsidies, shelters and special breaks,&#8221; most companies actually pay less than competitors abroad &#8212; often far less.</p>
<p>Mr. Kocieniewski&#8217;s investigation required sifting laboriously through mountains of regulatory filings and court cases to decode the taxes for dozens of American and foreign corporations. It also drew on federal lobbying records. The resulting articles exposed many of Corporate America&#8217;s tax secrets.</p>
<p>In April 2012, the series was <a href=http://www.pulitzer.org/citation/2012-Explanatory-Reporting>awarded a Pulitzer Prize for explanatory journalism. </a>The Pulitzer jury said Mr. Kocieniewski&#8217;s work &#8220;penetrated a legal thicket to explain how the nation&#8217;s wealthiest citizens and corporations often exploited loopholes and avoided taxes.&#8221; </p>" />

                    <div class="section" data-parent="navId-80E6DEE6-87E4-4AD0-9152-14FA6B07E5AB">

        <div id="subHdr" class="clearfix">
        <h1>But Nobody Pays That</h1>
    </div><!-- close subHdr --><div id="topic">
    <div class="abColumns">   
                <div class="wideA">
            <div id="story"><div class="story-meta"><div class="kicker"></div></div></div><!-- switchpoint -->        
            <div class="aColumn">
                <div id="ledeModule" class="columnGroup">
        <div class="summary"><p><a href="http://topics.nytimes.com/topics/reference/timestopics/people/k/david_kocieniewski/index.html">David Kocieniewski</a>, a business reporter for The New York Times, devoted a year to digging out and exposing the obscure provisions that businesses and the wealthiest Americans exploit to drive their tax bills down to rock bottom.</p>
<p>In a series called &#8220;But Nobody Pays That,&#8221; Mr. Kocieniewski showed how federal tax law takes with one hand but gives &#8212; generously &#8212; with the other.</p>
<p>At 35 percent, the corporate tax rate is nominally among the world&#8217;s highest. Yet because of &#8220;a bounty of subsidies, shelters and special breaks,&#8221; most companies actually pay less than competitors abroad &#8212; often far less.</p>
<p>Mr. Kocieniewski&#8217;s investigation required sifting laboriously through mountains of regulatory filings and court cases to decode the taxes for dozens of American and foreign corporations. It also drew on federal lobbying records. The resulting articles exposed many of Corporate America&#8217;s tax secrets.</p>
<p>In April 2012, the series was <a href="http://www.pulitzer.org/citation/2012-Explanatory-Reporting">awarded a Pulitzer Prize for explanatory journalism. </a>The Pulitzer jury said Mr. Kocieniewski&#8217;s work &#8220;penetrated a legal thicket to explain how the nation&#8217;s wealthiest citizens and corporations often exploited loopholes and avoided taxes.&#8221; </p></div>
    </div> <!-- end ledeModule -->
                                        
        
                                
                                	
	<div id="featuredArticles" class="columnGroup">
	<h3><![CDATA[]]></h3>
		    <div class="story clearfix">
                
	        	    <div class="callout">
    	    <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2011/12/30/business/tax-breaks-from-options-a-windfall-for-businesses.html">
    	    <img src="http://static01.nyt.com/images/2011/12/30/business/jp-OPTIONS/jp-OPTIONS-thumbStandard.jpg" alt="Tax Benefits From Options as Windfall for Businesses" width="75" height="75" border="0">
    	    </a>
    	    </div><!-- close callout -->
	    	    
	    <h4><a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2011/12/30/business/tax-breaks-from-options-a-windfall-for-businesses.html">Tax Benefits From Options as Windfall for Businesses</a></h4>
	    
	    	        <div class="byline">By DAVID KOCIENIEWSKI</div>
                
	    	        <p class="summary">Companies that granted stock options after the 2008 stock market collapse are benefiting from those awards too — in the form of tax savings.</p>
	    	    
	    	                	        <span class="date">December 30, 2011</span>
                
        <span class="section">business</span><span class="source">News</span>
	    </div><!-- close story clearfix -->
		    <div class="story clearfix">
                
	        	    <div class="callout">
    	    <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2011/11/27/business/estee-lauder-heirs-tax-strategies-typify-advantages-for-wealthy.html">
    	    <img src="http://static01.nyt.com/images/2011/11/27/business/lauder/lauder-thumbStandard.jpg" alt="A Family’s Billions, Artfully Sheltered" width="75" height="75" border="0">
    	    </a>
    	    </div><!-- close callout -->
	    	    
	    <h4><a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2011/11/27/business/estee-lauder-heirs-tax-strategies-typify-advantages-for-wealthy.html">A Family’s Billions, Artfully Sheltered</a></h4>
	    
	    	        <div class="byline">By DAVID KOCIENIEWSKI</div>
                
	    	        <p class="summary">Ronald S. Lauder, an heir to the Estée Lauder fortune, uses an aggressive tax strategy to preserve holdings that Forbes magazine says make him the world’s 362nd wealthiest person.</p>
	    	    
	    	                	        <span class="date">November 27, 2011</span>
                
        <span class="section">business</span><span class="source">News</span>
	    </div><!-- close story clearfix -->
		    <div class="story clearfix">
                
	        	    <div class="callout">
    	    <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2011/09/11/technology/rich-tax-breaks-bolster-video-game-makers.html">
    	    <img src="http://static01.nyt.com/images/2011/09/11/us/JP-GAMERS-2/JP-GAMERS-2-thumbStandard-v2.jpg" alt="Rich Tax Breaks Bolster Makers of Video Games" width="75" height="75" border="0">
    	    </a>
    	    </div><!-- close callout -->
	    	    
	    <h4><a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2011/09/11/technology/rich-tax-breaks-bolster-video-game-makers.html">Rich Tax Breaks Bolster Makers of Video Games</a></h4>
	    
	    	        <div class="byline">By DAVID KOCIENIEWSKI</div>
                
	    	        <p class="summary">A collection of tax deductions, write-offs and credits makes video game development one of the most highly subsidized businesses in the United States, a tax professor says.</p>
	    	    
	    	                	        <span class="date">September 11, 2011</span>
                
        <span class="section">technology</span><span class="source">News</span>
	    </div><!-- close story clearfix -->
		    <div class="story clearfix">
                
	        	    <div class="callout">
    	    <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2011/06/20/business/20tax.html">
    	    <img src="http://static01.nyt.com/images/2011/06/18/business/video-0620-nobody-pays-that/video-0620-nobody-pays-that-thumbStandard.jpg" alt="Companies Push for Tax Break on Foreign Cash" width="75" height="75" border="0">
    	    </a>
    	    </div><!-- close callout -->
	    	    
	    <h4><a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2011/06/20/business/20tax.html">Companies Push for Tax Break on Foreign Cash</a></h4>
	    
	    	        <div class="byline">By DAVID KOCIENIEWSKI</div>
                
	    	        <p class="summary">Some large corporations are pushing for a tax holiday to bring back offshore profits, but the economic benefits were limited the last time such a break was given.</p>
	    	    
	    	                	        <span class="date">June 20, 2011</span>
                
        <span class="section">business</span><span class="source">News</span>
	    </div><!-- close story clearfix -->
		    <div class="story clearfix">
                
	    	    
	    <h4><a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2011/05/03/business/economy/03rates.html">U.S. Business Has High Tax Rates but Pays Less</a></h4>
	    
	    	        <div class="byline">By DAVID KOCIENIEWSKI</div>
                
	    	        <p class="summary">The United States has the second-highest corporate tax rate in the world, but a myriad of loopholes make that no better than a sticker rate.</p>
	    	    
	    	                	        <span class="date">May 3, 2011</span>
                
        <span class="section">business</span><span class="source">News</span>
	    </div><!-- close story clearfix -->
		    <div class="story clearfix">
                
	    	    
	    <h4><a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2011/05/03/business/economy/03poll.html">Americans Favor Budget Cuts Over Raising Corporate Tax</a></h4>
	    
	    	        <div class="byline">By MARJORIE CONNELLY</div>
                
	    	        <p class="summary">A survey also found a majority of Republicans and independents favor cutting government spending over raising taxes on businesses, while Democrats are evenly divided.</p>
	    	    
	    	                	        <span class="date">May 3, 2011</span>
                
        <span class="section">business</span><span class="source">News</span>
	    </div><!-- close story clearfix -->
		    <div class="story clearfix">
                
	    	    
	    <h4><a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2011/03/25/business/economy/25tax.html">G.E.’s Strategies Let It Avoid Taxes Altogether</a></h4>
	    
	    	        <div class="byline">By DAVID KOCIENIEWSKI</div>
                
	    	        <p class="summary">General Electric is skilled at avoiding taxes, lately to the extent that the Treasury owes the company money.</p>
	    	    
	    	                	        <span class="date">March 25, 2011</span>
                
        <span class="section">business</span><span class="source">News</span>
	    </div><!-- close story clearfix -->

                                        <h3 class="kicker">
                        <span class="kicker-label"><a href="http://www.nytimes.com/pages/opinion/index.html#sundayreview">SundayReview</a></span>                            <span class="pipe">|</span>                        Op-Ed Columnist                </h3>
                        	<h1 itemprop="headline" id="story-heading" class="story-heading">A Rain of Bombs in the Nuba Mountains</h1>
                        <div id="story-meta-footer" class="story-meta-footer">
                                    <div class="simple-extended-byline">
    <div class="simple-thumb">
        <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/column/nicholas-kristof">            <img src="http://static01.nyt.com/images/2014/11/01/opinion/kristof-circular/kristof-circular-thumbLarge-v4.png" title="Nicholas Kristof" />

        <p class="simple-byline-column has-single-author">
                    <a class="simple-byline-column-link" href="http://www.nytimes.com/column/nicholas-kristof">Nicholas Kristof</a>
                <span class='simple-byline-date'>JUNE 20, 2015</span>
    </p>

        <p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="233" data-total-count="233" itemprop="articleBody" id="story-continues-1">IN THE NUBA MOUNTAINS, Sudan — THE eight children in the Shanta family lived in a village of grass huts and had never turned on an electric light or worn a watch, never ridden a car or even a bicycle, never spoken on the telephone.</p><aside id='middle-right-paid-post-container' class='ad middle-right-ad paid-post-ad hidden nocontent robots-nocontent'>
                                <h2 class='marginalia-heading'></h2>
                                    <ul class='story-menu'>
                                        <li id='MiddleRightPaidPost1' class='story-menu-item ad'></li>
                                        <li id='MiddleRightPaidPost2' class='story-menu-item ad'></li>
                                        <li id='MiddleRightPaidPost3' class='story-menu-item ad'></li>
                                        <li id='MiddleRightPaidPost4' class='story-menu-item ad'></li>
                                    </ul>
                                </aside><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="390" data-total-count="623" itemprop="articleBody" id="story-continues-2">Their first real interaction with the 21st century came one February night as they huddled in a foxhole while the Sudanese government shelled their hut. If you want to understand why President Omar Hassan al-Bashir of Sudan <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2015/06/16/world/africa/omar-hassan-al-bashir-sudan-south-africa.html">should be prosecuted by the International Criminal Court</a>, the Shanta family offers a reminder that Bashir’s atrocities will continue as long as he remains in power.</p><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="114" data-total-count="737" itemprop="articleBody">The shell landed near the huts of the extended Shanta family, instantly killing a female cousin, Amusa Shanta, 18.</p><aside class="marginalia collection-marginalia collection collection-type-column collection-tone-opinion collection-section-opinion collection-theme-latest-headlines nocontent robots-nocontent" role="complementary">
    <div class="nocontent robots-nocontent">
                <header>
                        <div class="thumb">
                <img src="http://static01.nyt.com/images/2014/11/01/opinion/kristof-circular/kristof-circular-blogSmallThumb-v2.png" alt="" />
            </div>
                        <div class="collection-meta">
                <div class="follow-button-placeholder" data-collection-id="column.nicholas-kristof"></div>
                <h2 class="collection-marginalia-heading"><a href="http://www.nytimes.com/column/nicholas-kristof">Nicholas Kristof</a></h2>
                <h3 class="collection-marginalia-subheading">Human rights, women’s rights, health, global affairs.</h3>
            </div>
        </header>
        <ul>
                        <li><article class="story theme-summary">
    <a class="story-link" href="http://www.nytimes.com/2015/11/26/opinion/donald-trump-meet-a-syrian-refugee-named-heba.html?rref=collection%2Fcolumn%2FNicholas Kristof">
        <h2 class="story-heading">
            <span class="story-heading-text">
                                Donald Trump, Meet a Syrian Refugee Named Heba            </span>
            <time class="dateline" datetime="2015-11-26">NOV 26</time>
        </h2>
    </a>
</article>
</li>
                        <li><article class="story theme-summary">
    <a class="story-link" href="http://www.nytimes.com/2015/11/22/opinion/sunday/the-statue-of-liberty-must-be-crying-with-shame.html?rref=collection%2Fcolumn%2FNicholas Kristof">
        <h2 class="story-heading">
            <span class="story-heading-text">
                                ‘The Statue of Liberty Must Be Crying With Shame’            </span>
            <time class="dateline" datetime="2015-11-21">NOV 21</time>
        </h2>
    </a>
</article>
</li>
                        <li><article class="story theme-summary">
    <a class="story-link" href="http://www.nytimes.com/2015/11/19/opinion/betraying-ourselves.html?rref=collection%2Fcolumn%2FNicholas Kristof">
        <h2 class="story-heading">
            <span class="story-heading-text">
                                They Are Us            </span>
            <time class="dateline" datetime="2015-11-19">NOV 19</time>
        </h2>
    </a>
</article>
</li>
                        <li><article class="story theme-summary">
    <a class="story-link" href="http://www.nytimes.com/2015/11/15/opinion/sunday/gifts-with-meaning.html?rref=collection%2Fcolumn%2FNicholas Kristof">
        <h2 class="story-heading">
            <span class="story-heading-text">
                                Gifts With Meaning            </span>
            <time class="dateline" datetime="2015-11-14">NOV 14</time>
        </h2>
    </a>
</article>
</li>
                        <li><article class="story theme-summary">
    <a class="story-link" href="http://www.nytimes.com/2015/11/12/opinion/mizzou-yale-and-free-speech.html?rref=collection%2Fcolumn%2FNicholas Kristof">
        <h2 class="story-heading">
            <span class="story-heading-text">
                                Mizzou, Yale and Free Speech            </span>
            <time class="dateline" datetime="2015-11-11">NOV 11</time>
        </h2>
    </a>

            <p class="user-action"><a href="http://www.nytimes.com/column/nicholas-kristof">See More &raquo;</a></p>

</aside>
<p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="530" data-total-count="1267" itemprop="articleBody">Inside the foxhole, the children survived the initial blast. But the explosion set fire to a nearby hut, and its grass roof slid into their foxhole while aflame. These foxholes are deep earthen pits beside almost every home, school and building here in the Nuba Mountains so that people can crouch for protection when the government bombs them — as it does almost daily, in one village or another. The depth offers protection from shrapnel, but it also makes it difficult to escape in cases like this when they become fire pits.</p><figure id="Newsletter-embed-Kristof" class="interactive interactive-embedded  has-adjacency has-lede-adjacency limit-small layout-small">
    <a class="visually-hidden skip-to-text-link" href="#story-continues-3">Continue reading the main story</a>

    
    <figcaption class="interactive-caption">
            </figcaption>

        <div class="interactive-graphic">

<div id="d-promo-realestate">
	<h2 class="d-headline">Nicholas Kristof's Newsletter</h2>
	<p class="d-leadin">Sign up to receive my emails about each column and other occasional commentary.</p>

<p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="182" data-total-count="1449" itemprop="articleBody" id="story-continues-3">“They screamed,” recalled Osman Shanta, the father of five of the children in that foxhole, who was frantically throwing water on the blaze. “And then the screaming stopped.”</p><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="328" data-total-count="1777" itemprop="articleBody">Two of his children — Diana, 12, and Bashir, 9 — were immediately burned to death, but his three other children survived, along with three cousins. A car rushed the six children four hours over a dirt track to a hospital; even that can be perilous, because <a href="http://www.doctorswithoutborders.org/article/sudan-msf-hospital-bombed-south-kordofan">Sudan also bombs both vehicles and hospitals</a> in the Nuba Mountains.</p><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="59" data-total-count="1836" itemprop="articleBody">So why was Sudan shelling a village filled with grass huts?</p><aside class="marginalia comments-marginalia  featured-comment-marginalia" data-marginalia-type="sprinkled" data-skip-to-para-id="story-continues-4">
</aside>
<p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="144" data-total-count="1980" itemprop="articleBody" id="story-continues-4">This was not an accident of war. It reflects a deliberate Sudanese scorched-earth, counterinsurgency strategy here in the southern end of Sudan.</p><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="385" data-total-count="2365" itemprop="articleBody">A rebel army with many thousands of soldiers, seemingly strongly supported by local people as their protector from the national government, governs the Nuba Mountains. The Sudanese government bombs the rebels and periodically attacks them, but the majority of its attacks seem to target civilians, apparently to make the area uninhabitable so that no one is left to support the rebels.</p><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="269" data-total-count="2634" itemprop="articleBody"><a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2012/02/23/opinion/kristof-dodging-bombers-in-sudan.html">This is my fourth visit</a> <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2012/06/03/opinion/sunday/kristof-starving-its-own-children.html">to the Nuba Mountains</a>, all surreptitious, because Sudan bans journalists, aid workers and just about everyone else from this area. I slipped in through the rebel lines in a vehicle smeared with mud so that it would be harder for bombers to spot.</p><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="292" data-total-count="2926" itemprop="articleBody">The staff at the hospital alerted me to the six burned Shanta children. “They were burned horrendously when they arrived,” remembers <a href="http://time.com/3823233/tom-catena-2015-time-100/">Dr. Tom Catena, an American who is the lone physician at the hospital</a>. A 3-year-old girl, Ayat, almost half her body charred, died within a couple of days.</p><aside class="marginalia comments-marginalia  comment-prompt-marginalia" data-marginalia-type="sprinkled" data-skip-to-para-id="story-continues-5">
</aside>
<p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="124" data-total-count="3050" itemprop="articleBody" id="story-continues-5">A sister, Hosana, 10, looked as if she might make it, but then the wounds became infected with tetanus and she died as well.</p><div id="MiddleRight1" class="ad ad-placeholder nocontent robots-nocontent"><div class="accessibility-ad-header visually-hidden">
    <p>Advertisement</p>
</div>
<a class="visually-hidden skip-to-text-link" href="#story-continues-6">Continue reading the main story</a>
</div>
    	<div id="MiddleRightN" class="ad text-ad middle-right-ad nocontent robots-nocontent"><div class="accessibility-ad-header visually-hidden">
    <p>Advertisement</p>
</div>
<a class="visually-hidden skip-to-text-link" href="#story-continues-6">Continue reading the main story</a>
</div><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="403" data-total-count="3453" itemprop="articleBody" id="story-continues-6">Dr. Catena focused on trying to save their 8-year-old brother, Shanta, who persevered for weeks. But flies were laying eggs in his wounds, and soon the burns were crawling with maggots. Dr. Catena says that he would cut out the maggots, and the next day more would return. The boy showed extraordinary courage, Dr. Catena remembers, but he would scream every day from pain as his dressings were changed.</p><aside class="marginalia comments-marginalia  selected-comment-marginalia" data-marginalia-type="sprinkled" data-skip-to-para-id="story-continues-7">
</aside>
<p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="26" data-total-count="3479" itemprop="articleBody" id="story-continues-7">Eventually, he, too, died.</p><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="317" data-total-count="3796" itemprop="articleBody">I reached the Shanta family’s village after driving through a no-man’s land of bombed homes, and I found traumatized villagers staying near caves where they shelter from bombings. The three surviving Shanta children are literally scarred for life; one girl, Rhoda, 6, can’t extend her arms because of the burns.</p><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="163" data-total-count="3959" itemprop="articleBody">Osman Shanta and his wife, Sadia, grieve for their lost four children, as well as their nieces. They comfort their surviving son, Abdullah, when he has nightmares.</p><button class="button comments-button  theme-speech-bubble" data-skip-to-para-id="story-continues-8">
</button>
<p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="158" data-total-count="4117" itemprop="articleBody" id="story-continues-8">On the day I visited, the village was bombed yet again. The rainy season is beginning now, which means that the caves are wet, so the children are often sick.</p><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="494" data-total-count="4611" itemprop="articleBody">A few groups — <a href="http://nubareports.org/">Nuba Reports</a>, <a href="http://www.enoughproject.org/">the Enough Project</a>, <a href="http://www.hrw.org/africa/sudan">Human Rights Watch</a> — call attention to the suffering here, but the Nuba Mountains have no strategic value and neither the United States nor other governments have made much of an issue of the bombings, or of the lack of humanitarian access. As a senator, President Obama criticized President George W. Bush for acquiescing in Sudanese atrocities, but <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2010/08/29/opinion/29kristof.html">the Obama administration has done less to pressure Sudan</a> than the Bush administration did.</p><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="157" data-total-count="4768" itemprop="articleBody">That single shell killed six cousins in one family, and left three children disfigured. And these bombs and shells continue to fall, day after day after day.</p>
        
                                        <footer class="story-footer story-content">
    <div class="story-meta">
                        <div class="story-info"><p>I invite you to sign up for my free, twice-weekly newsletter. <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/newsletters/nicholas-kristof/">When you do</a>, you’ll receive an email about my columns as they’re published and other occasional commentary. <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/newsletters/nicholas-kristof/">Sign up here.<br /><br /></a>I also invite you to visit my blog, <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/ontheground">On the Ground</a>. Please also join me on <a href="http://www.facebook.com/kristof">Facebook</a> and <a href="https://plus.google.com/102839963139173448834/posts?hl=en">Google+</a>, watch my <a href="http://www.youtube.com/nicholaskristof">YouTube videos</a> and follow me on <a href="http://twitter.com/nickkristof">Twitter</a>.</p></div>

        
        <p class="story-print-citation">A version of this op-ed appears in print on June 21, 2015, on page SR11 of the <span itemprop="printEdition">New York edition</span> with the headline: A Rain of Bombs. <span class="story-footer-links">  <span><a href="http://www.nytimes.com/pages/todayspaper/index.html" target="_blank">Today's Paper</a><span class="pipe">|</span></span><span><a href="http://www.nytimes.com/subscriptions/Multiproduct/lp839RF.html?campaignId=48JQY" target="_blank">Subscribe</a></span>
</span>
</p>
    </div><!-- close story-meta -->
    </footer>

</article>

	        {% set img_src_attr = 'src' %}
	        {% if sponsorPlaylist %}
			{% set video_url = video.seo_url|playlistOverride(video.playlist.id) %}
			{% else %}
			{% set video_url = video.seo_url|playlistOverride(playlist.id) %}
			{% endif %}
	  
  
	

	{% if video.playlist.type|getPlaylistType() === "show" %}
		{% set inChannel = false %}
		{% set inShow = true %}
	{% else %}
		{% if video.playlist.type|getPlaylistType() === "channel" %}
			{% set inChannel = true %}
			{% set inShow = false %}
		{% else %}
			{% set inChannel = false %}
			{% set inShow = false %}
		{% endif %}
	{% endif %}

	{% set itemPropURL = '' %}
	{% set itemPropContentURL = '' %}
	{% set itemPropName = '' %}
	{% set itemPropListElement = '' %}
	{% set itemScope = '' %}
	{% if pageType !== "watch" %}
		{% set itemPropURL = 'itemprop=url' %}
		{% set itemPropContentURL = 'itemprop=contentUrl' %}
		{% set itemPropName = 'itemprop=name' %}
		{% set itemPropListElement = 'itemprop=associatedMedia' %}
		{% set itemScope = 'itemscope itemtype=http://schema.org/ImageObject' %}
	{% endif %}

	<li data-id="{{ video.id }}"
		class="thumb-item {{ visible_state }} {% if !hideShowKicker %}show-enabled{% endif %} {{video.assetMeta.name}} {% if inShow %}channel-name-{{ video.playlist.parent_channel.headline | slugify | default('default') }} channel-id-{{ video.playlist.parent_channel.id }} {% else %} channel-name-default {% endif %} channel-themed" {{ itemPropListElement }} {{ itemScope }} >

		{% if !hideShowKicker %}
		<span class="show-logo">
			{% if inShow %}<a class="show-name-link" href="{{ video.playlist.publish_url|getPlaylistLabel() }}">{{ video.playlist.headline }}</a>{% else %}<span class="show-name-link">&nbsp;</span>{% endif %}
		</span>
		{% endif %}
		<a data-collection="{{playlist.headline}}" class="thumb-holder" href="{{ video_url }}" {{ itemPropURL }}>
			<span class="thumb-wrapper">
				<span class="thumb-border"></span>
				<span class="thumb-crop">
					<img class="thumb-img" alt="{{ video.headline }}" title="{{ video.headline }}" {{ img_src_attr }}="{{ 'images'|getDomain }}{{ video.images | getThumbImage(moduleType, pageType, default_images) }}" {{ itemPropContentURL }} />
				</span>
			</span>
			<div class="play-holder">
				<p class="button-play"><i class="times-sprite icon {{video.assetMeta.name}}"></i></p>
			</div>
		</a>
		{# To do using video metadata add link to the channel/section #}
		{% if !hideChannelKicker %}
		<h5 class="thumb-section-name">
			{% if inShow %}
				<a class="section-name-link" href="{{ video.playlist.parent_channel.publish_url|getPlaylistLabel() }}">{{ video.playlist.parent_channel.headline }}</a>
			{% else %}
				<a class="section-name-link" href="{{ video.playlist.publish_url|getPlaylistLabel() }}">{{ video.playlist.headline }}</a>
			{% endif %}

                        	<h1 itemprop="headline" id="story-heading" class="story-heading">The Lasting Power of Dr. King’s Dream Speech</h1>
                        <div id="story-meta-footer" class="story-meta-footer">
                                    <div class="simple-extended-byline simple-no-thumb">

        <p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="320" data-total-count="320" itemprop="articleBody" id="story-continues-1">It was late in the day and hot, and after a long march and an afternoon of speeches about federal legislation, unemployment and racial and social justice, the Rev. Dr. <a href="http://topics.nytimes.com/top/reference/timestopics/people/k/martin_luther_jr_king/index.html?inline=nyt-per" title="More articles about Martin Luther King Jr.." class="meta-per">Martin Luther King Jr.</a> finally stepped to the lectern, in front of the Lincoln Memorial, to address the crowd of 250,000 gathered on the National Mall.</p><aside id='middle-right-paid-post-container' class='ad middle-right-ad paid-post-ad hidden nocontent robots-nocontent'>
                                <h2 class='marginalia-heading'></h2>
                                    <ul class='story-menu'>
                                        <li id='MiddleRightPaidPost1' class='story-menu-item ad'></li>
                                        <li id='MiddleRightPaidPost2' class='story-menu-item ad'></li>
                                        <li id='MiddleRightPaidPost3' class='story-menu-item ad'></li>
                                        <li id='MiddleRightPaidPost4' class='story-menu-item ad'></li>
                                    </ul>
                                </aside><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="502" data-total-count="822" itemprop="articleBody" id="story-continues-2">He began slowly, with magisterial gravity, talking about what it was to be black in America in 1963 and the “shameful condition” of race relations a hundred years after the <a href="http://topics.nytimes.com/top/reference/timestopics/subjects/c/civil_war_us_/emancipation_proclamation/index.html?inline=nyt-classifier" title="More articles about Emancipation Proclamation." class="meta-classifier">Emancipation Proclamation</a>. Unlike many of the day’s previous speakers, he did not talk about particular bills before Congress or the marchers’ demands. Instead, he situated the civil rights movement within the broader landscape of history — time past, present and future — and within the timeless vistas of Scripture.</p>    <aside class="marginalia related-coverage-marginalia nocontent robots-nocontent" data-marginalia-type="sprinkled" role="complementary" module="RelatedCoverage-Marginalia">
        <div class="nocontent robots-nocontent">
            <a class="visually-hidden skip-to-text-link" href="#story-continues-3">Continue reading the main story</a>

<p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="580" data-total-count="1402" itemprop="articleBody" id="story-continues-3">Dr. King was about halfway through his prepared speech when Mahalia Jackson — who earlier that day had delivered a stirring rendition of the spiritual “I Been ’Buked and I Been Scorned” — shouted out to him from the speakers’ stand: “Tell ’em about the ‘Dream,’ Martin, tell ’em about the ‘Dream’!” She was referring to a riff he had delivered on earlier occasions, and Dr. King pushed the text of his remarks to the side and began an extraordinary improvisation on the dream theme that would become one of the most recognizable refrains in the world.</p><figure id="media-100000002408615" class="media photo embedded has-adjacency has-lede-adjacency layout-large-vertical media-100000002408615" data-media-action="modal" itemprop="associatedMedia" itemscope itemid="http://static01.nyt.com/images/2013/08/28/us/sub-jp-KINGSPEECH-2/sub-jp-KINGSPEECH-2-blog427.jpg" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" aria-label="media" role="group">
    <span class="visually-hidden">Photo</span>
    <div class="image">
            <img src="http://static01.nyt.com/images/2013/08/28/us/sub-jp-KINGSPEECH-2/sub-jp-KINGSPEECH-2-blog427.jpg" alt="" class="media-viewer-candidate" data-mediaviewer-src="http://static01.nyt.com/images/2013/08/28/us/sub-jp-KINGSPEECH-2/sub-jp-KINGSPEECH-2-superJumbo.jpg" data-mediaviewer-caption="Words spoken that day by Dr. King still reverberate." data-mediaviewer-credit="Associated Press"  itemprop="url" itemid="http://static01.nyt.com/images/2013/08/28/us/sub-jp-KINGSPEECH-2/sub-jp-KINGSPEECH-2-blog427.jpg"/>
            <meta itemprop="height" content="638" />
            <meta itemprop="width" content="427" />
    </div>
        <figcaption class="caption" itemprop="caption description">
                <span class="caption-text">Words spoken that day by Dr. King still reverberate.</span>
                        <span class="credit" itemprop="copyrightHolder">
            <span class="visually-hidden">Credit</span>
            Associated Press        </span>
            </figcaption>
    </figure>
<p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="506" data-total-count="1908" itemprop="articleBody">With his improvised riff, Dr. King took a leap into history, jumping from prose to poetry, from the podium to the pulpit. His voice arced into an emotional crescendo as he turned from a sobering assessment of current social injustices to a radiant vision of hope — of what America could be. “I have a dream,” he declared, “my four little children will one day live in a nation where they will not be judged by the color of their skin but by the content of their character. I have a dream today!”</p><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="790" data-total-count="2698" itemprop="articleBody">Many in the crowd that afternoon, 50 years ago on Wednesday, had taken buses and trains from around the country. Many wore hats and their Sunday best — “People then,” the civil rights leader John Lewis would recall, “when they went out for a protest, they dressed up” — and the Red Cross was passing out ice cubes to help alleviate the sweltering August heat. But if people were tired after a long day, they were absolutely electrified by Dr. King. There was reverent silence when he began speaking, and when he started to talk about his dream, they called out, “Amen,” and, “Preach, Dr. King, preach,” offering, in the words of his adviser Clarence B. Jones, “every version of the encouragements you would hear in a Baptist church multiplied by tens of thousands.”</p><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="281" data-total-count="2979" itemprop="articleBody">You could feel “the passion of the people flowing up to him,” James Baldwin, a skeptic of that day’s March on Washington, later wrote, and in that moment, “it almost seemed that we stood on a height, and could see our inheritance; perhaps we could make the kingdom real.”</p>    <aside class="marginalia related-coverage-marginalia nocontent robots-nocontent" data-marginalia-type="sprinkled" role="complementary" module="RelatedCoverage-Marginalia">
        <div class="nocontent robots-nocontent">
            <a class="visually-hidden skip-to-text-link" href="#story-continues-4">Continue reading the main story</a>

        </div><!-- close nocontent -->

<p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="589" data-total-count="3568" itemprop="articleBody" id="story-continues-4">Dr. King’s speech was not only the heart and emotional cornerstone of the March on Washington, but also a testament to the transformative powers of one man and the magic of his words. Fifty years later, it is a speech that can still move people to tears. Fifty years later, its most famous lines are recited by schoolchildren and sampled by musicians. Fifty years later, the four words “I have a dream” have become shorthand for Dr. King’s commitment to freedom, social justice and nonviolence, inspiring activists from Tiananmen Square to Soweto, Eastern Europe to the West Bank.</p><div id="MiddleRight1" class="ad ad-placeholder nocontent robots-nocontent"><div class="accessibility-ad-header visually-hidden">
    <p>Advertisement</p>
</div>
<a class="visually-hidden skip-to-text-link" href="#story-continues-5">Continue reading the main story</a>
</div><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="566" data-total-count="4134" itemprop="articleBody" id="story-continues-5">Why does Dr. King’s “Dream” speech exert such a potent hold on people around the world and across the generations? Part of its resonance resides in Dr. King’s moral imagination. Part of it resides in his masterly oratory and gift for connecting with his audience — be they on the Mall that day in the sun or watching the speech on television or, decades later, viewing it online. And part of it resides in his ability, developed over a lifetime, to convey the urgency of his arguments through language richly layered with biblical and historical meanings.</p><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="752" data-total-count="4886" itemprop="articleBody">The son, grandson and great-grandson of Baptist ministers, Dr. King was comfortable with the black church’s oral tradition, and he knew how to read his audience and react to it; he would often work jazzlike improvisations around favorite sermonic riffs — like the “dream” sequence — cutting and pasting his own words and those of others. At the same time, the sonorous cadences and ringing, metaphor-rich language of the King James Bible came instinctively to him. Quotations from the Bible, along with its vivid imagery, suffused his writings, and he used them to put the sufferings of African-Americans in the context of Scripture — to give black audience members encouragement and hope, and white ones a visceral sense of identification.</p><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="550" data-total-count="5436" itemprop="articleBody">In his “Dream” speech, Dr. King alludes to a famous passage from Galatians, when he speaks of “that day when all of God’s children — black men and white men, Jews and Gentiles, Protestants and Catholics — will be able to join hands.” As he did in many of his sermons, he also drew parallels between “the Negro” still an “exile in his own land” and the plight of the Israelites in Exodus, who, with God on their side, found deliverance from hardship and oppression, escaping slavery in Egypt to journey toward the Promised Land.</p><aside class="marginalia comments-marginalia  featured-comment-marginalia" data-marginalia-type="sprinkled" data-skip-to-para-id="story-continues-6">
</aside>
<p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="903" data-total-count="6339" itemprop="articleBody" id="story-continues-6">The entire March on Washington speech reverberates with biblical rhythms and parallels, and bristles with a panoply of references to other historical and literary texts that would have resonated with his listeners. In addition to allusions to the prophets Isaiah (“I have a dream that one day every valley shall be exalted, every hill and mountain shall be made low”) and Amos (“We will not be satisfied until justice rolls down like waters and righteousness like a mighty stream”), there are echoes of the <a href="http://www.archives.gov/exhibits/charters/declaration_transcript.html">Declaration of Independence</a> (“the unalienable rights of life, liberty and the pursuit of happiness”); Shakespeare (“this sweltering summer of the Negro’s legitimate discontent”); and popular songs like Woody Guthrie’s “This Land Is Your Land” (“Let freedom ring from the mighty mountains of New York,” “Let freedom ring from the curvaceous slopes of California”).</p><figure id="media-100000002408531" class="media photo embedded has-adjacency layout-large-vertical media-100000002408531" data-media-action="modal" itemprop="associatedMedia" itemscope itemid="http://static01.nyt.com/images/2013/08/28/us/KINGSPEECH/KINGSPEECH-blog427.jpg" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" aria-label="media" role="group">
    <span class="visually-hidden">Photo</span>
    <div class="image">
            <img src="http://static01.nyt.com/images/2013/08/28/us/KINGSPEECH/KINGSPEECH-blog427.jpg" alt="" class="media-viewer-candidate" data-mediaviewer-src="http://static01.nyt.com/images/2013/08/28/us/KINGSPEECH/KINGSPEECH-superJumbo.jpg" data-mediaviewer-caption="Today, Dr. King's famous words are chipped into the spot where he spoke." data-mediaviewer-credit="Pablo Martinez Monsivais/Associated Press"  itemprop="url" itemid="http://static01.nyt.com/images/2013/08/28/us/KINGSPEECH/KINGSPEECH-blog427.jpg"/>
            <meta itemprop="height" content="683" />
            <meta itemprop="width" content="427" />
    </div>
        <figcaption class="caption" itemprop="caption description">
                <span class="caption-text">Today, Dr. King's famous words are chipped into the spot where he spoke.</span>
                        <span class="credit" itemprop="copyrightHolder">
            <span class="visually-hidden">Credit</span>
            Pablo Martinez Monsivais/Associated Press        </span>
            </figcaption>
    </figure>
<p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="1051" data-total-count="7390" itemprop="articleBody">Such references added amplification and depth of field to the speech, much the way T. S. Eliot’s myriad allusions in “The Waste Land” add layered meaning to that poem. Dr. King, who had a doctorate in theology and once contemplated a career in academia, was shaped by both his childhood in his father’s church and his later studies of disparate thinkers like Reinhold Niebuhr, Gandhi and Hegel. Along the way, he developed a gift for synthesizing assorted ideas and motifs and making them his own — a gift that enabled him to address many different audiences at once, while making ideas that some might find radical somehow familiar and accessible. It was a gift that in some ways mirrored his abilities as the leader of the civil rights movement, tasked with holding together often contentious factions (from more militant figures like Stokely Carmichael to more conservative ones like Roy Wilkins), while finding a way to balance the concerns of grass-roots activists with the need to forge a working alliance with the federal government.</p><div id="Moses" class="ad moses-ad nocontent robots-nocontent"><div class="accessibility-ad-header visually-hidden">
    <p>Advertisement</p>
</div>
<a class="visually-hidden skip-to-text-link" href="#story-continues-7">Continue reading the main story</a>
</div><div id="MiddleRight2" class="ad ad-placeholder nocontent robots-nocontent"><div class="accessibility-ad-header visually-hidden">
    <p>Advertisement</p>
</div>
<a class="visually-hidden skip-to-text-link" href="#story-continues-7">Continue reading the main story</a>
</div><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="728" data-total-count="8118" itemprop="articleBody" id="story-continues-7">At the same time, Dr. King was also able to nestle his arguments within a historical continuum, lending them the authority of tradition and the weight of association. For some, in his audience, the articulation of his dream for America would have evoked conscious or unconscious memories of Langston Hughes’s call in a 1935 poem to “let America be the dream the dreamers dreamed” and W. E. B. Du Bois’s description of the “wonderful America, which the founding fathers dreamed.” His final lines in the March on Washington speech come from a Negro spiritual reminding listeners of slaves’ sustaining faith in the possibility of liberation: “Free at last, free at last; thank God Almighty, we are free at last.”</p><aside class="marginalia comments-marginalia  selected-comment-marginalia" data-marginalia-type="sprinkled" data-skip-to-para-id="story-continues-8">
</aside>
<p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="762" data-total-count="8880" itemprop="articleBody" id="story-continues-8">For those less familiar with African-American music and literature, there were allusions with immediate, patriotic connotations. Much the way Lincoln redefined the founders’ vision of America in his <a href="http://www.abrahamlincolnonline.org/lincoln/speeches/gettysburg.htm">Gettysburg Address</a> by invoking the Declaration of Independence, so Dr. King in his “Dream” speech makes references to both the Gettysburg Address and the Declaration of Independence. These deliberate echoes helped universalize the moral underpinnings of the civil rights movement and emphasized that its goals were only as revolutionary as the founding fathers’ original vision of the United States. Dr. King’s dream for America’s “citizens of color” was no more, no less than the American Dream of a country where “all men are created equal.”</p><aside class="marginalia comments-marginalia  comment-prompt-marginalia" data-marginalia-type="sprinkled" data-skip-to-para-id="story-continues-9">
</aside>
<p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="642" data-total-count="9522" itemprop="articleBody" id="story-continues-9">As for Dr. King’s quotation of “My Country, ’Tis of Thee” — an almost de facto national anthem, familiar even to children — it underscored civil rights workers’ patriotic belief in the project of reinventing America. For Dr. King, it might have elicited personal memories, too. The night his home was bombed during the bus boycott in Montgomery, Ala., endangering the lives of his wife, Coretta, and their infant daughter, he calmed the crowd gathered in front of their house, saying, “I want you to love our enemies.” Some of his supporters reportedly broke into song, including hymns and “My Country, ’Tis of Thee.”</p><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="579" data-total-count="10101" itemprop="articleBody">The March on Washington and Dr. King’s “Dream” speech would play an important role in helping pass the 1964 Civil Rights Act, and the pivotal Selma to Montgomery march that he led in 1965 would provide momentum for the passage later that year of the <a href="http://topics.nytimes.com/topics/reference/timestopics/subjects/v/voting_rights_act_1965/index.html?inline=nyt-classifier" title="More articles about the Voting Rights Act." class="meta-classifier">Voting Rights Act</a>. Though Dr. King received the <a href="http://www.nobelprize.org/nobel_prizes/peace/laureates/1964/">Nobel Peace Prize in 1964</a>, his exhausting schedule (he had been giving hundreds of speeches a year) and his frustration with schisms in the civil rights movement and increasing violence in the country led to growing weariness and depression before his assassination in 1968.</p><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="767" data-total-count="10868" itemprop="articleBody">The knowledge that Dr. King gave his life to the cause lends an added poignancy to the experience of hearing his speeches today. And so does being reminded now — in the second term of Barack Obama’s presidency — of the dire state of race relations in the early 1960s, when towns in the South still had separate schools, restaurants, hotels and bathrooms for blacks and whites, and discrimination in housing and employment was prevalent across the country. Only two and a half months before the “Dream” speech, Gov. George Wallace had stood in a doorway at the University of Alabama in an attempt to block two black students from trying to register; the next day the civil rights activist<a href="http://www.eversinstitute.org/"> Medgar Evers </a>was assassinated in front of his home in Jackson, Miss.</p><button class="button comments-button  theme-speech-bubble" data-skip-to-para-id="story-continues-10">
</button>
<div id="MiddleRight3" class="ad ad-placeholder nocontent robots-nocontent"><div class="accessibility-ad-header visually-hidden">
    <p>Advertisement</p>
</div>
<a class="visually-hidden skip-to-text-link" href="#story-continues-10">Continue reading the main story</a>
</div>
    	<div id="MiddleRightN" class="ad text-ad middle-right-ad nocontent robots-nocontent"><div class="accessibility-ad-header visually-hidden">
    <p>Advertisement</p>
</div>
<a class="visually-hidden skip-to-text-link" href="#story-continues-10">Continue reading the main story</a>
</div><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="335" data-total-count="11203" itemprop="articleBody" id="story-continues-10">President Obama, who once wrote about his mother’s coming home “with books on the civil rights movement, the recordings of Mahalia Jackson, the speeches of Dr. King,” has described the leaders of the movement as “giants whose shoulders we stand on.” Some of his own speeches owe a clear debt to Dr. King’s ideas and words.</p><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="774" data-total-count="11977" itemprop="articleBody">In his 2004 Democratic National Convention keynote address, which brought him to national attention, Mr. Obama channeled Dr. King’s vision of hope, speaking of coming “together as one American family.” In his 2008 speech about race, he talked, much as Dr. King had, of continuing “on the path of a more perfect union.” And in his 2007 speech commemorating the 1965 Selma march, he echoed Dr. King’s remarks about Exodus, describing Dr. King and the other civil rights leaders as members of the Moses generation who “pointed the way” and “took us 90 percent of the way there.” He and his contemporaries were their heirs, Mr. Obama said — they were members of the Joshua generation with the responsibility of finishing “the journey Moses had begun.”</p><p class="story-body-text story-content" data-para-count="663" data-total-count="12640" itemprop="articleBody">Dr. King knew it would not be easy to “transform the jangling discords of our nation into a beautiful symphony of brotherhood” — difficulties that persist today with new debates over voter registration laws and the Trayvon Martin shooting. Dr. King probably did not foresee a black president celebrating the 50th anniversary of his speech in front of the Lincoln Memorial, and surely did not foresee a monument to himself just a short walk away. But he did dream of a future in which the country embarked on “the sunlit path of racial justice,” and he foresaw, with bittersweet prescience, that 1963, as he put it, was “not an end, but a beginning.”</p>
        
                                        <footer class="story-footer story-content">
    <div class="story-meta">
                <div class="story-notes"><p>Follow Michiko Kakutani on Twitter: <a href="https://twitter.com/michikokakutani">@michikokakutani</a></p></div>

<h1 class="story-heading">The Show</h1>
<div class="intro">
  
</div>
      
          <article class="cp-block clear first" 
    data-idx="0" 
    data-short-url= "" 
    itemscope itemid="http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/projects/cp/oscars-2015/show/neil-patrick-harris-sings-jokes-strips" itemtype="http://schema.org/NewsArticle">
    <div class="cp-image filmstrip ">
        <figure id="media-100000003529089" class="photo media media-100000003529089 layout-large-horizontal ratio-tall" 
  itemscope 
  itemprop="associatedMedia image" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" itemid="http://i2.nyt.com/images/2015/02/23/arts/20150223MOMENTS-slide-ATYZ/20150223MOMENTS-slide-ATYZ-jumbo.jpg" 
  data-media-action="modal" 
  aria-label="media" role="group">
  <span class="visually-hidden">Photo</span>
  <div class="image">
    <img alt="" itemprop="url" 
        src="http://i2.nyt.com/images/2015/02/23/arts/20150223MOMENTS-slide-ATYZ/20150223MOMENTS-slide-ATYZ-jumbo.jpg" 
        class="media-viewer-candidate" 
        data-mediaviewer-src="http://i2.nyt.com/images/2015/02/23/arts/20150223MOMENTS-slide-ATYZ/20150223MOMENTS-slide-ATYZ-jumbo.jpg"

    </div>
    <div class="cp-text filmstrip " id="cp-block-54eab78061646d672e130000">
        <h6 class="cp-kicker"></h6>
        <h2 class="cp-celeb" itemprop="headline name">
    <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/projects/cp/oscars-2015/show/neil-patrick-harris-sings-jokes-strips" itemprop="url">Neil Patrick Harris Sings, Jokes, Strips</a>
</h2>

        <figcaption class="cp-desc" itemprop="articleBody caption description text">
            <p>A veteran host of other awards ceremonies, Neil Patrick Harris made his Oscar debut on the Dolby Theater stage and did a little bit of everything. There was a spectacle-packed opening number featuring the DeLorean time machine from “Back to the Future” and several Marilyn Monroes. And there was his parody of a scene from “Birdman,” in which Michael Keaton runs through Times Square in his underpants. Mr. Harris ran through backstage corridors and onto the stage in white briefs and black socks and shoes. </p><p>The show itself was an awkward mix of socially charged commentary — like Patricia Arquette’s call for gender equity and John Legend&#39;s plea to protect voting rights — and traditional segments like a tribute to  “The Sound of Music.”  After Laura Poitras collected the Oscar for her documentary “Citizenfour” and praised her subject, the whistleblower Edward J. Snowden, “for his courage,” Mr. Harris joked that he “couldn’t be here for some treason.”</p>
        </figcaption>
        <ul class="cp-related">
</ul>
            <div class="tone-standard sharetools theme-classic sharetools-story-meta layout-horizontal" 
   
  aria-label="tools" role="group" 
  data-url="http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/projects/cp/oscars-2015/show/neil-patrick-harris-sings-jokes-strips" 
  data-title="Oscars 2015 | The Show | Neil Patrick Harris Sings, Jokes, Strips" 
  data-description="A veteran host of other awards ceremonies, Neil Patrick Harris made his Oscar debut on the Dolby Theater stage and did a little bit of everything. There was a spectacle-packed opening number featuring the DeLorean time machine from “Back to the Future” and several Marilyn Monroes. And there was his parody of a scene from “Birdman,” in which Michael Keaton runs through Times Square in his underpants. Mr. Harris ran through backstage corridors and onto the stage in white briefs and black socks and shoes. 

The show itself was an awkward mix of socially charged commentary — like Patricia Arquette’s call for gender equity and John Legend&#39;s plea to protect voting rights — and traditional segments like a tribute to  “The Sound of Music.”  After Laura Poitras collected the Oscar for her documentary “Citizenfour” and praised her subject, the whistleblower Edward J. Snowden, “for his courage,” Mr. Harris joked that he “couldn’t be here for some treason.”
"

        <div class="cp-text photo " id="cp-block-54e8dfc061646d1d82020000">
            <h6 class="cp-date">
                <a class="cp-date-a" href="http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/projects/cp/oscars-2015/show/a-soggy-red-carpet">June 17, 2015</a>
            </h6>
            <h2 class="cp-celeb" itemprop="headline name">
    <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/projects/cp/oscars-2015/show/a-soggy-red-carpet" itemprop="url">A Soggy Red Carpet</a>
</h2>

            <div class="cp-desc" itemprop="articleBody description text">
                <p>With a 90 percent chance of rain, the Academy was taking no chances and erected elaborate tenting.  But a leak sprung and pandemonium ensued. Among the journalists. The celebrities were much more sanguine. For the British contingent, which included David Oyelowo, the weather was a reminder of home. “If the Brits are going to invade, we should let the weather do it, too,” he  said.</p><p>And Kerry Washington, the “Scandal” star, had practical advice for her fellow celebrities. “So what if we all have to deal with a little bit of sogginess and frizz?” she said. “California needs water. Everybody suck it up and celebrate the rain.”</p>
            </div>
            <ul class="cp-related">
</ul>
        </div>
        <div class="tone-standard sharetools theme-classic sharetools-story-meta layout-horizontal"

    <div class="cp-text filmstrip " id="cp-block-54eac34661646d3ead0b0000">
        <h6 class="cp-kicker"></h6>
        <h2 class="cp-celeb" itemprop="headline name">
    <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/projects/cp/oscars-2015/show/in-search-of-diversity" itemprop="url">In Search of Diversity (and Ratings)</a>
</h2>

        <figcaption class="cp-desc" itemprop="articleBody caption description text">
            <p>The Academy took steps Sunday  to quiet the clamor — fueled by the perceived snub of “Selma” in the directing and acting categories — over a lack of diversity among its membership voters. Among the black stars lined up to present were Terrence Howard, Kevin Hart, Oprah Winfrey,  Zoe Saldana and Viola Davis. The musical performers included Common, John Legend and Jennifer Hudson. </p><p>The Oscar audience was up last year, to 43.6 million viewers, the best since 2000 according to Nielsen. But that set the bar high — and the black audience is crucial. In a good ratings year, like 2005, when Chris Rock was the host, African-Americans accounted for 12.5 percent of Oscar viewers, Nielsen data showed. In a weak year, like 2008, they fell to 6.7 percent. </p><p>Neil Patrick Harris hit the controversy head on at the start of the show, quipping, “Tonight we honor Hollywood’s best and whitest, sorry, brightest.” </p><p>— BROOKS BARNES and MICHAEL CIEPLY</p>
        </figcaption>
        <ul class="cp-related">
</ul>
            <div class="tone-standard sharetools theme-classic sharetools-story-meta layout-horizontal" 
   
  aria-label="tools" role="group" 
  data-url="http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/projects/cp/oscars-2015/show/in-search-of-diversity" 
  data-title="Oscars 2015 | The Show | In Search of Diversity (and Ratings)" 
  data-description="The Academy took steps Sunday  to quiet the clamor — fueled by the perceived snub of “Selma” in the directing and acting categories — over a lack of diversity among its membership voters. Among the black stars lined up to present were Terrence Howard, Kevin Hart, Oprah Winfrey,  Zoe Saldana and Viola Davis. The musical performers included Common, John Legend and Jennifer Hudson. 

The Oscar audience was up last year, to 43.6 million viewers, the best since 2000 according to Nielsen. But that set the bar high — and the black audience is crucial. In a good ratings year, like 2005, when Chris Rock was the host, African-Americans accounted for 12.5 percent of Oscar viewers, Nielsen data showed. In a weak year, like 2008, they fell to 6.7 percent. 

Neil Patrick Harris hit the controversy head on at the start of the show, quipping, “Tonight we honor Hollywood’s best and whitest, sorry, brightest.” 

— BROOKS BARNES and MICHAEL CIEPLY
"

        <h2 class="cp-celeb" itemprop="headline name">
    <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/projects/cp/oscars-2015/show/patricia-arquette-s-rousing-speech" itemprop="url">Patricia Arquette’s Impassioned Speech for Equal Rights</a>
</h2>

        <figcaption class="cp-desc" itemprop="articleBody caption description text">
            <p>Gender equity, or lack thereof, was a theme of this awards season, and the absence of female filmmakers like Ava DuVernay (“Selma”) and Angelina Jolie (“Unbroken”) in the best director category was widely noted. So when Patricia Arquette took the stage to accept her best supporting actress Oscar, her rousing speech  brought cheers from the audience and a fellow nominee, Meryl Streep, to her feet. “To every woman who gave birth, to every taxpayer and citizen of this nation, we have fought for everybody’s equal rights,” Ms. Arquette said. “It’s our time to have wage equality once and for all, and equal rights for women in the United States of America.”</p>
        </figcaption>

        <h2 class="cp-celeb" itemprop="headline name">
    <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/projects/cp/oscars-2015/show/the-lego-oscars" itemprop="url">The Lego Oscars</a>
</h2>

        <figcaption class="cp-desc" itemprop="articleBody caption description text">
            <p>“The Lego Movie” may have gotten snubbed for best animated feature, and it may have lost best song, the only category it was nominated in. But the performance of that song, “Everything Is Awesome,” was a hit in the Dolby Theater and online. </p><p>The rousing number featured Tegan and Sara, the Lonely Island guys, Questlove, a Batman, many many dancing construction workers  (per the movie) and even a furry Awesome Possum. </p><p>But they weren’t even the best part: That would be the Lego Oscars given to Oprah and others and were instantly coveted by multitudes of Twitter users. </p><p>By the way: Not many people knew Tegan and Sara were behind the song. “It’s kind of like the perfect thing where we can tell people it’s us if we want them to know,” Sara said on the red carpet, “and then if they’re like, that song is driving me crazy, I’m like, I know, who sings it, they’re the worst.”</p>

    <div class="cp-text filmstrip " id="cp-block-54eaaabf61646d67320e0000">
        <h6 class="cp-kicker"></h6>
        <h2 class="cp-celeb" itemprop="headline name">
    <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/projects/cp/oscars-2015/show/joan-rivers" itemprop="url">Joan Rivers Is Left Out of the In Memoriam Tribute</a>
</h2>

        <figcaption class="cp-desc" itemprop="articleBody caption description text">
            <p>For the In Memoriam segment of the Academy Awards, the membership of the committee that selects who is remembered  reportedly <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2013/02/08/movies/awardsseason/even-the-oscar-memorial-list-is-a-focus-of-campaigning.html">keeps itself secret</a>. After Joan Rivers was not remembered in this year&#39;s broadcast, you could understand why.</p><p>The segment mentioned Mike Nichols, Maya Angelou, Elizabeth Peña and more than one marketing executive. But when Ms. Rivers wasn&#39;t mentioned, the outrage was vented in many, many tweets: </p><p><em>&quot;Did I miss Joan Rivers? Or did the Oscars?&quot; — <a href="https://twitter.com/poniewozik/status/569702862957219840">@poniewozik</a></em></p><p><em>&quot;Hey @Oscars Joan Rivers is the only reason 3/4ths of people watch your boring 3 hour award show, put her in the In Memoriam.&quot; — <a href="https://twitter.com/nikkikoppers/status/569703639901609984">@nikkikoppers</a></em></p><p><em>&quot;Look, Joan Rivers was ok but she was no Marketing Executive. — <a href="https://twitter.com/BillCorbett/status/569704526040551424">@BillCorbett</a></em></p><p><em>&quot;Bad news, Academy. There&#39;s a red carpet on the way to the afterlife, and Joan Rivers will show you even less mercy than usual.&quot; — <a href="https://twitter.com/peterhartlaub/status/569704131264323584">@peterhartlaub</a></em></p><p>Ms. Rivers, as the chief of fashion police for the red carpet, played an important role in the Oscars, but perhaps one  the Academy might not want to acknowledge.  </p><p>— MICHAEL ROSTON </p>
        </figcaption>
        <ul class="cp-related">
</ul>
            <div class="tone-standard sharetools theme-classic sharetools-story-meta layout-horizontal" 
   
  aria-label="tools" role="group" 
  data-url="http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/projects/cp/oscars-2015/show/joan-rivers" 
  data-title="Oscars 2015 | The Show | Joan Rivers Is Left Out of the In Memoriam Tribute" 
  data-description="For the In Memoriam segment of the Academy Awards, the membership of the committee that selects who is remembered  reportedly keeps itself secret. After Joan Rivers was not remembered in this year&#39;s broadcast, you could understand why.

The segment mentioned Mike Nichols, Maya Angelou, Elizabeth Peña and more than one marketing executive. But when Ms. Rivers wasn&#39;t mentioned, the outrage was vented in many, many tweets:

    <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/projects/cp/oscars-2015/show/travolta" itemprop="url">Travolta Gets the Name Right, but What's With the Face Touching?</a>
</h2>

        <figcaption class="cp-desc" itemprop="articleBody caption description text">
            <p>John Travolta memorably mangled Idina Menzel’s name at last year’s ceremony. Remember Adele Dazeem? But the witty idea of pairing the two onstage at this year’s show was undercut when the actor touched her face in a sort-of caress more than once. Cue the <a href="http://mashable.com/2015/02/22/idina-menzel-john-travolta-oscars/">GIFs</a>.</p>

        <figcaption class="cp-desc" itemprop="articleBody caption description text">
            <p>Common and John Legend’s performance of “Glory,” the song they wrote for “Selma,” proved an emotional showstopper. As a fitting capper, the musicians won the best song Oscar. </p>

            <h2 class="cp-celeb" itemprop="headline name">
    <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/projects/cp/oscars-2015/show/gaga" itemprop="url">Lady Gaga and Julie Andrews, Together at Last</a>
</h2>

            <div class="cp-desc" itemprop="articleBody description text">
                <p>The Oscar producers Craig Zadan and Neil Meron are known for their love of movie musicals, and they’ve used the Academy Awards ceremony to celebrate the form before. This year, they enlisted Lady Gaga in a 50th-anniversary tribute to “The Sound of Music” that proved winning even if it arrived after 11 p.m. At the end, the original Maria, Julie Andrews, applauded her note-perfect performance.</p>
